Many startups that fail do so because the problem was invented, not discovered.

**The organic path to startup ideas is to get good at something, work on hard problems, and pay close attention to what frustrates you. The idea generation is a side effect of deep engagement with a domain — not the output of a separate ideation process. This is why the best founders typically have genuine expertise rather than generalist opportunism.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** The fastest path to a great startup idea may be to spend a year becoming genuinely expert at something difficult and paying obsessive attention to what's broken. Domain expertise combined with frustration tolerance is a more reliable engine for opportunity discovery than any structured ideation method.

**Ideas that seem obvious to the founder but aren't obvious to others are often the most valuable.** Graham calls this the 'obvious to us, unknown to them' asymmetry. It usually exists because the founder has lived through an experience or works in a domain that most investors and observers haven't — which makes the idea look risky from the outside when it's actually de-risked by insider knowledge. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Don't abandon an idea just because outsiders don't immediately get it. If you understand why the idea is good in a way that requires real experience to appreciate, that asymmetry is valuable. The goal is to be right, not to be immediately understood by a general audience.

**Graham notes that startup ideas exist on a spectrum from 'things that seem good and are' to 'things that seem bad but are good.' The most dangerous quadrant is things that seem good but aren't — they waste years of founder time and investor capital. The most underexplored quadrant is things that seem bad but are actually good — and that's where outlier returns come from.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Develop a practice of examining the ideas in your space that smart people have dismissed. Ask whether the dismissal was based on genuine domain knowledge or on pattern-matching to something superficially similar. A defensible reason why the conventional wisdom is wrong is the core of a great investment thesis — or a great startup.

**Graham describes a class of opportunities he calls 'new things that are underrated because they're new.' When a new technology or behavior first appears, it is systematically underestimated because most people evaluate it using frameworks built for what came before. The founders who correctly assess the new thing early — not after it's been validated — are the ones who build category-defining companies.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Treat emerging technologies and behaviors as objects of serious study, not curiosities to revisit later when they're more mature. The right time to engage deeply is when the technology still looks like a toy. By the time it looks like an opportunity, the early advantage is gone.

**One reliable method for finding startup ideas is to look at things that have recently changed — in technology, regulation, culture, or infrastructure — and ask what those changes make newly possible. Change creates temporary windows where the established players haven't adapted and the new entrant has a structural advantage. Startups are built for windows, not equilibria.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Build a regular practice of identifying changes in your environment — new platforms, new regulations, new consumer behaviors — and immediately asking what they unlock. The startup opportunity lives in the gap between the change and the market's adaptation to it, and that gap closes faster than founders expect.

**According to Graham, the most dangerous version of the question 'is this a good idea?' is when it's answered by committee or consensus. Ideas that survive social evaluation tend to be the ones that are legible, safe, and already validated — which are exactly the properties that make an idea less likely to be a great startup. Good ideas often fail social validation tests before they succeed in the market.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Be extremely cautious about running your startup idea through informal networks for approval before stress-testing it against real users. Advisors and peers optimize for plausibility; customers optimize for need. These are different filters and often produce opposite verdicts.

**Graham's framework treats the startup idea as an artifact of the founder's experience and position, not as a free-floating object to be discovered through research. The question isn't 'what ideas are out there?' but 'given who I am, what I know, and what I've experienced, what problem am I uniquely positioned to see and solve?' This reframes idea generation as identity and positioning work.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Invest in understanding your own unique vantage point before searching for ideas. What industries have you worked in? What tools have frustrated you? What do you understand that most people don't? Your competitive advantage as a founder starts with the specificity of your position, not the quality of your brainstorming.

**Graham has noted that the ideas that become the biggest companies often require the founder to believe something specific about the future that most people don't believe yet. This is not vague optimism — it's a precise, falsifiable belief about how the world is changing. The founder's job in the early stage is to be right about that belief before the market validates it.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com))

**Implication:** Articulate the specific belief about the future that your startup depends on. Make it precise and falsifiable. If you can't state what you believe that others don't, and why you're likely to be right, you don't yet have the intellectual core of a great startup — regardless of how well the product is built.

**The way Graham thinks about startup ideas is temporal.** live at the leading edge of a technology or domain, notice what is missing or broken, and realize that the future everyone else will eventually want is already visible to you now. The best founders are not predicting the future — they are already living in it and building for the world around them that others haven't arrived at yet. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com/startupideas.html))

**Implication:** The most reliable way to generate startup ideas is to push yourself to the frontier of technology and stay there. Subscribe to the most obscure technical journals, use the most experimental tools, be among the first adopters. The gap between where you live and where most people live is where startup ideas hide.

**The best startup ideas are not generated by sitting down to brainstorm startup ideas — that process produces ideas that sound plausible but lack genuine demand. Instead, the best ideas emerge from living at the frontier of a technology or domain, noticing something that is missing or broken, and realizing that other people must be experiencing the same problem. The idea finds you; you do not find it.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com/startupideas.html))

**Implication:** The most productive way to generate startup ideas is to immerse yourself deeply in a field, build things for yourself, and pay attention to what frustrates you. Trying to reverse-engineer a startup idea from market size or trend reports produces systematically worse results.

**Schlep blindness — the unconscious filtering out of startup ideas that involve tedious, unglamorous, or unpleasant work — is one of the biggest sources of missed entrepreneurial opportunity. Smart founders instinctively avoid ideas that seem like a drag, but their disgust at the schlep is itself a signal that others share it and would pay someone to handle it. The willingness to embrace the schlep is a genuine competitive advantage.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas — paulgraham.com/startupideas.html))

**Implication:** When evaluating startup ideas, explicitly look for the ones you dismissed because they seemed annoying or boring to execute. The tedium that repels other founders is your moat. Some of the most valuable companies were built by people willing to do the work that everyone else found beneath them.

**The web app was invented not out of vision but out of laziness — a desire to avoid learning Windows.** The founders of Viaweb realized they could run software on the server and let users control it through a browser, not because they foresaw the future of the web, but because they refused to learn Microsoft's platform. ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

> *"We were really really highly motivated to figure out how to write software without having to write software to run on Windows... I sat up in bed when I thought of this... oh my god we could avoid having to write software on Windows at all."*

**Implication:** Some of the most transformative startup ideas come from aversion or constraint rather than grand vision — the desire to avoid a problem can be as generative as the desire to solve one.

**Sometimes the most important product insight is recognizing that a category can be fundamentally redefined rather than just competed in. Jambox succeeded not because it was a better speaker but because the team reframed it as a networked computer for your phone — a new category that existing speaker companies couldn't see because they were inside the old one.** ([source](foundation:27_hosain_rahman))

> *"Half the organization here thought we were crazy... we were like no no we're going to turn the thing into a computer and it's going to be for your phone and it's going to be for Spotify and it's going to be a totally new world and we were able to like carve out this new space."*

**Implication:** The best hardware opportunities often involve redefining what a device category is, not building a marginally better version of what already exists — category creation beats category competition.

**Great startup ideas often begin not with a business plan but with a genuine obsession with a user interface problem.** Jawbone's founders started from a visceral frustration with the limitations of Palm Pilot's graffiti input while mobile, and asked what a better solution would look like — this consumer-first question led them toward voice recognition years before Siri. ([source](foundation:27_hosain_rahman))

> *"Our view our vision was could we try to create essentially like Siri... a voice controlled interface for mobile devices... which would be on top of the next generation of graphical... this is kind of like 96 97."*

**Implication:** Founders who obsess over a real interaction problem and follow it seriously — even if the technology isn't ready — are often years ahead of the market and well-positioned when enabling technology catches up.

**Building in a domain where you are a genuine heavy user gives you authentic insight into the problem.** Domain expertise built through personal use — even without formal credentials — is a legitimate foundation for a startup. ([source](youtube:Nozomio_Arlan))

> *"I'm a very heavy user of GitHub Copilot and cursor. I really want to work in that space like the codegen developer tools because I didn't really have like domain expertise outside of that. I'm still at high school. I only know how to code."*

**Implication:** Founders don't need industry credentials to have real insight — being a power user of a product category is sufficient to develop the intuition needed to improve it.

**Viaweb built a general-purpose shopping search engine called 'Shop Find' at the end of 1997, featuring a programmable crawler that could index products from stores across the web. This was advanced technology for the era, years before comparison shopping became mainstream.** ([source](snapshot-viaweb-june-1998))

> *"At the end of 1997 we released a general purpose shopping search engine called Shop Find. It was pretty advanced for the time — it had a programmable crawler that could crawl most of the different stores online and pick out the products."*

**Implication:** Viaweb was not just building merchant tools but was developing adjacent technology that anticipated the comparison shopping category — founders who build infrastructure often see adjacent opportunities earlier than pure product companies.

**When conducting user interviews, the goal is to discover not just what users think is wrong, but what is actually missing in their lives. Users often describe surface symptoms rather than root problems, and the interviewer must dig to find the underlying need they may not consciously recognize.** ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

**Implication:** User interviews that take stated complaints at face value produce incremental improvements; interviews that probe for underlying unmet needs can reveal entirely new product directions the user couldn't have articulated.

**The build-versus-buy decision in enterprise AI follows a clear logic.** companies build AI internally only for their core competency, where every marginal performance gain is strategically critical. Everything else — horizontal workflows that are important but not differentiating — is a buy opportunity for third-party AI vendors. ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"Build is one of companies really building something that's their core competency right so like you could take the most extreme view and say Google Search... and you know that's not really an opportunity for third-party vendors... on the buy side though the lens we use is where are their horizontal business workflows that are important but not the core competency of any one organization."*

**Implication:** AI startup founders should consciously position in horizontal workflows that cross industry verticals, avoiding domains where their largest potential customers will always prioritize internal development over vendor solutions.

**The best way to get startup ideas is not to brainstorm them but to notice problems you personally experience.** The most successful startups — Microsoft, Apple, Yahoo, Google, Facebook — all began with founders solving problems they themselves had. This personal connection ensures the problem actually exists. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"The way to get startup ideas is not to try to think of startup ideas. It's to look for problems, preferably problems you have yourself."*

**Implication:** Founders should stop running ideation sessions and instead pay close attention to friction in their own daily lives. Personal pain points are the most reliable signal that a real problem exists.

**The fact that OpenAI was originally started as a nonprofit reveals how small AI seemed in 2015.** No one then thought AI would be worth spending vast sums on; the nonprofit structure was evidence of limited ambition, not deception. ([source](Paul Graham, Founder, Y Combinator — Part 2))

> *"Nobody knew then cuz this was like 2015 or something when he started OpenAI, right? Nobody knew then that it would be worth spending so much on AI... the fact that you would even think you could start you could even do this as a you could be an AI thing as a nonprofit. It just shows you how small it seemed back then. You could fund it with donations. Right? Ridiculous."*

**Implication:** The scale of transformative technologies is almost always underestimated at inception. Structural decisions made at founding — like nonprofit status — can haunt institutions for decades when the opportunity turns out to be much larger than expected.

**Continuous health monitoring and early diagnosis — knowing about heart disease or cancer before symptoms appear — will seem obvious and essential to future generations, just as antibiotics seem obvious to us now. Currently, even the most powerful and well-resourced people must wait for symptoms before diagnosis, which is a massive and unnecessary failure.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

> *"I am pretty sure that 50 or 100 years from now it will seem barbaric to people that we had to wait until we had symptoms to be diagnosed with things like heart disease and cancer... Bill Clinton has access to the best medical care available, and even he had to wait until his arteries were 90% blocked to learn that the number was 90%."*

**Implication:** The ongoing health monitoring space represents one of the largest potential startup opportunities, combining technical difficulty, regulatory challenge, and enormous human value — the hallmarks of a great hard problem.

**A large idea always has people nibbling at its edges without realizing how large the full opportunity is.** The startups working on pieces of a big problem are often unaware of the total size of what they're attacking. ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Founders working in a large problem space should periodically zoom out to assess the full scope of what they're building toward — the adjacent opportunity may be much larger than the immediate product suggests.

**A 'sitcom startup idea' — like a social network for pet owners — sounds reasonable and gets polite responses from friends, but generates zero actual users. The danger is that people say 'maybe I could see using that' rather than 'I need this now,' and the aggregate of lukewarm interest across millions of people still equals no one urgently using the product.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"They don't want to use it themselves, at least not right now, but they could imagine other people wanting it. Sum that reaction across the entire population, and you have zero users."*

**Implication:** Friendly, non-committal feedback from potential users is a red flag, not a green light. Founders should test for urgency, not just openness. 'I could see using that someday' is a rejection in disguise.

**The 'consult for one user' strategy — picking a single user and acting as if you're a consultant building only for them — is a powerful way to find product-market fit. Once you've made something one user needs perfectly, you've usually made something others want too.** ([source](paulgraham.com/ds.html))

**Implication:** Deep focus on solving one user's problem completely is more productive than trying to broadly satisfy many users vaguely — specificity of solution often generalizes better than expected.

**There can be an entirely unrecognized market segment hiding in plain sight — in ClassPass's case, 'fitness dabblers' who wanted variety rather than loyalty to one studio. Conventional wisdom had only served loyalists; the dabbler market was completely unserved.** ([source](Masters of Scale: How to price your product to scale (with ClassPass' Payal Kadakia)))

> *"There was this market out there for dabblers when everyone was trying to push everyone to be like complete loyalists... there was only sort of the gym for the dabbler."*

**Implication:** Founders should question the implicit assumptions their industry makes about who the customer is — unserved adjacent segments can be larger and more passionate than the assumed core market.

**In 1998, the search engine landscape was dominated by seven players — Yahoo, AltaVista, Excite, WebCrawler, Infoseek, Lycos, and HotBot — with Google not yet incorporated. The incumbents seemed definitive at the time, yet were soon displaced.** ([source](snapshot-viaweb-june-1998))

**Implication:** Even when a market appears settled and complete, transformative new entrants can emerge and render the existing map obsolete — humility about 'settled' industries is warranted.

**Planned meetings may be systematically worse than unplanned ones because planning requires having a reason in advance, which filters out high-variance, unexpected connections — the very ones most likely to be transformative. Unplanned meetings allow rapid, real-time filtering based on genuine shared interest.** ([source](youtube:Paul_Graham_Stockholm))

> *"Maybe planned meetings are too conservative. Maybe planned meetings lop off the outliers, just like deliberately trying to find startup ideas lops off the outliers. You have to have a reason for the meeting beforehand, and that makes you too conservative."*

**Implication:** Structuring your environment to maximize unplanned collisions with interesting people may be more valuable than optimizing your calendar with intentional networking.

**Natural language will become the dominant interface for computing.** The current paradigm of navigating software through menus and structured UI is fundamentally clunky; large language models combined with actuation capabilities will allow users to express intent in natural language and have AI systems execute complex multi-step workflows on their behalf. ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"One thing that we've talked a lot about in Silicon Valley is this idea of like a natural language interface to Computing in many ways the way we use computers is super clunky right... you'll have tools that will be able to do things like hey my mom's birthday is next Wednesday can you please organize a gift."*

**Implication:** The natural language interface represents a platform shift as significant as the graphical user interface — startups that build natively for this interaction model rather than retrofitting it onto existing UI paradigms will have a structural advantage.

**The combination of large language models with program synthesis and actuation capabilities — the ability to not just understand but to take action — is an emerging frontier that will enable AI systems to automate complex multi-step enterprise workflows, not just answer questions.** ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"The combination of these large language models with program synthesis and the ability to take action on behalf of users I think is something that we're beginning to see early signs of... imagine you have models that can understand but then actually act on behalf of the end user in an Enterprise context take actions in business applications to kind of automate workflows."*

**Implication:** The next generation of enterprise AI products will be defined not by what they can understand or generate but by what they can do — founders who combine reasoning with actuation are building in the most valuable part of the stack.

**The best startup ideas often have a quality of seeming naughty or outrageous — like they're exploiting a loophole or doing something that shouldn't even be possible. This feeling of transgressive cleverness is a positive signal, not a warning.** ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

**Implication:** When evaluating startup ideas, founders and investors should pay attention to ideas that feel slightly illicit or impossible — that quality often signals genuine novelty rather than mere weirdness.

**One of the highest-leverage things PG did in office hours was expand founders' vision of what their startup could become — roughly one-third of office hours involved identifying how to make an idea much bigger.** ([source](Paul Graham, Founder, Y Combinator — Part 2))

> *"I would do it like maybe 1/3 of the office hours I did. We would figure out how to make an idea much bigger. Yeah, it happened all the time and still does happen. Yeah, when I do office hours now, I still do that. And I see these startups right in the beginning of the batch sometimes and so there's a lot of room to expand their idea."*

**Implication:** Early-stage founders systematically underestimate the potential scope of their ideas. A key function of great mentorship is expanding the founder's conception of what's possible before they've prematurely optimized for a smaller version.

**AI infrastructure for the enterprise ML pipeline — spanning data management, model training, serving, monitoring, and explainability — represents a durable and largely underbuilt investment category. Each stage of the pipeline is a discrete opportunity as enterprises professionalize their AI operations.** ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"If you think about the pipeline to actually building a machine learning application starting at data management and then doing things like model Discovery training serving monitoring there has been a lot that's happened over the last 18 to 24 months both in terms of new companies and open source projects that make each step of the pipeline much much easier."*

**Implication:** Enterprise AI infrastructure is not a winner-take-all market but a collection of distinct wedges — specialized companies that own a specific stage of the pipeline can build durable businesses as AI operations mature inside large organizations.

**Cybersecurity is one of the most fertile domains for discriminative AI applications because the fundamental problem — finding signal in massive volumes of telemetry — is precisely what AI classification models excel at. The domain's data abundance and the cost of false negatives make the ROI case for AI unusually clear.** ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"On the Cyber side we think it's extremely fertile because in cyber you have so much Telemetry and so much noise to sift through and like the discriminative abilities of AI are actually really useful in kind of parsing the signal there."*

**Implication:** Founders looking for enterprise AI wedges should prioritize domains characterized by data abundance, high cost of errors, and incumbent solutions built on rules-based approaches — cybersecurity is the canonical example of all three.

**The democratization of AI for non-technical business users — analogous to how Tableau made data analysis accessible to people without SQL skills — is a major infrastructure opportunity. The goal is enabling domain experts with predictive problems to harness AI without needing to understand the underlying technical implementation.** ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"I think about the analogy of if you take Tableau and lots of people inside companies know how to use Tableau to get a sense of what's happened in the past in their data and what's the equivalent for what's going to happen in the future and so if I have a business house who may not know what a hyper parameter is but has a predictive problem can we build tooling to actually enable those people to harness the power of AI."*

**Implication:** The largest addressable market for enterprise AI tools is not data scientists but the far larger population of domain experts with business problems — products that abstract away ML complexity while preserving predictive power will scale faster than technical tools.

**For investors, only contrarian insights have value.** If a prediction about a company is widely shared, the stock price already reflects it, leaving no profit opportunity. The same logic applies to startup founders — starting a company in a space everyone agrees is good means competitors are already there. ([source](How to Think for Yourself))

**Implication:** For founders and investors alike, consensus validation is a red flag, not a green light. The most valuable opportunities look like bad ideas to most people.

**The best startup ideas don't come from trying to start a startup — they come from working on projects you find genuinely interesting. YC itself was an example of this: it started as an experiment, not a deliberate company-building effort.** ([source](youtube:Studio1.0_YC_2014))

> *"The best way to start a startup that's really going to take over the world is don't even try to start a startup just try and start a project that you think would be cool to work on and YC was an instance of this."*

**Implication:** Founders who are solving a problem they care about intrinsically are more likely to persist and build something meaningful than those optimizing for startup success from the outset.

**There is enormous room for improvement in consumer products that are widely used but deeply painful — such as flight search. The existence of widespread pain without a good solution is a startup opportunity signal.** ([source](youtube:MrStartupPaulGraham))

> *"Boy is there a problem where there's room for improvement right — like I actually buy fewer plane tickets because it is so painful to buy them right."*

**Implication:** User pain that leads to avoidance behavior — not just frustration — is a strong signal of opportunity. If people are opting out of a market because the experience is too bad, the product gap is real.

**Trying to consciously think up startup ideas is not just unproductive — it's actively dangerous.** It produces ideas that sound plausible enough to fool you into working on them, but have no real demand. Graham calls these 'made-up' or 'sitcom' startup ideas. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"That m.o. is doubly dangerous: it doesn't merely yield few good ideas; it yields bad ideas that sound plausible enough to fool you into working on them."*

**Implication:** Plausibility is a trap. An idea that sounds reasonable to everyone but is urgently needed by no one is worse than an obviously bad idea, because it wastes years of your life before revealing itself as hollow.

**Good startup ideas come from 'external stimulus hitting a prepared mind.' Bill Gates heard about the Altair and immediately saw an opportunity for a Basic interpreter. Drew Houston forgot his USB stick and thought 'I need my files online.' Many people experienced the same stimuli — only prepared minds converted them into companies.** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"If you look at the way successful founders have had their ideas, it's generally the result of some external stimulus hitting a prepared mind."*

**Implication:** Preparation — through deep domain immersion, technical skill, and active observation — is what converts ordinary experiences into company-founding insights. The 'lucky' founder is usually just the most prepared one.

**Being at the 'leading edge' of a field doesn't require being a builder or researcher — you can be there as a power user.** Zuckerberg saw the opportunity for Facebook not primarily because he was a programmer, but because he already 'lived online' when most adults found the concept of sharing their lives publicly horrifying. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"It was not so much because he was a programmer that Facebook seemed a good idea to Mark Zuckerberg as because he used computers so much. If you'd asked most 40 year olds in 2004 whether they'd like to publish their lives semi-publicly on the Internet, they'd have been horrified at the idea. But Mark already lived online; to him it seemed natural."*

**Implication:** Intense, habitual use of a technology or domain can qualify someone as a leading-edge observer just as much as building it. Non-technical founders can access great ideas by becoming the most advanced users in a category.

**Young founders who build from side projects are more trustworthy than those who brainstorm startup ideas, because side projects organically validate that a real problem exists. Without life experience, young founders trying to deliberately invent startup ideas are more likely to produce bullshit.** ([source](Paul Graham on Ambition, Art, and Evaluating Talent))

> *"Part of the reason you want to find young founders who've done stuff from side projects is that it guarantees the idea is not bullshit. Because if young founders sit down and try to think of a start-up idea, it's more likely to be bullshit because they don't have any experience of the world."*

**Implication:** Young founders should resist the urge to 'think up' startup ideas and instead build things they personally need. The side project as origin story is a quality signal, not just a narrative.

**Health and self-improvement have unusual universal market properties.** unlike most consumer technology categories where significant segments of people simply don't care, virtually every person — regardless of fitness level — expresses a desire to get incrementally better. This makes health the most defensible mass market hook for wearable technology. ([source](foundation:27_hosain_rahman))

> *"I've never been in a category like this where every single person I talk to... every single person cares and they all want to get better no matter if they're a superhuman athlete or they're just out of shape... wherever they are starting from they all want to sort of get incrementally better."*

**Implication:** Health is the rare category where the total addressable market is genuinely universal — founders building wearables should anchor the product value proposition in health improvement even if the device serves other functions.

**The most successful startups often look like nonprofits in their early phases, and many ideas that seem like they should be nonprofits would actually make excellent companies. The two converge because genuine user value and commercial success are deeply aligned.** ([source](youtube:startup-school-08-paul-graham))

> *"Very successful startups often behave like nonprofits. And when you think about nonprofit ideas, surprisingly often, they'd make good companies. Whichever direction you start from, you end up at the same place."*

**Implication:** Founders can use the 'would this make a good nonprofit?' test as a signal for whether an idea is genuinely valuable — and if it would, it may also make a great company.

**The best way to find startup ideas is to look for problems, not solutions.** Noticing things that are broken in your own life or work naturally leads to solutions — the Octopart founders found their idea because they needed to buy components for their own research. ([source](youtube:startup-school-08-paul-graham))

> *"I think the best way to look for ideas is look for problems. Don't try and think of solutions. Just try and find good problems and let them drive the solutions. Just look for stuff that seems broken."*

**Implication:** Idea generation should be problem-led, not solution-led — founders who are embedded in a domain will notice broken things that outsiders cannot, and those observations are the raw material of good startups.

**Starting with a narrow, specific market segment — like Facebook starting with Harvard students — creates conditions for rapid critical mass. The targeted audience feels the product was made specifically for them, making adoption far easier than trying to appeal to everyone at once.** ([source](youtube:do-things-that-dont-scale-pg))

> *"Facebook did this with Harvard. The students felt like it was specifically for them. And like kindling, it was much easier to start than say, first convincing your aunt to stream her political views to the world."*

**Implication:** Founders who try to target everyone from day one create a product that resonates with no one — finding the smallest viable market segment and dominating it is the correct ignition strategy.

**Starting in a narrow market is worth doing not just for adoption ease but because it validates the product before attempting to scale. A subset of the market where you can achieve critical mass quickly is a proving ground, not a limitation.** ([source](youtube:do-things-that-dont-scale-pg))

> *"It's always worth asking if there's a subset of the market in which you can get a critical mass of users quickly."*

**Implication:** Early market focus is both a growth tactic and a validation mechanism — it lets founders confirm product-market fit before committing resources to broad distribution.

**The best startup ideas live just on the right side of impossible.** The challenge is that at the moment you encounter them, it's genuinely hard to tell whether they're on the right side or the wrong side of that line. This ambiguity is part of what makes them frightening. ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

> *"The best ideas are right on just the right side of impossible. And it's hard to tell at the point where you have them whether they're on the right side or the wrong side."*

**Implication:** Founders should not dismiss ideas simply because they seem nearly impossible — that quality is a feature, not a bug, of the best startup ideas.

**The way to attack a gigantic startup idea is not to confront it head-on, but to find the tiny seed — the 'dinosaur egg' — that grows into it. For a search engine to rival Google, that seed is making a search engine that the top 10,000 hackers love, even if no one else uses it. A small but powerful initial user base in the right position is enormously valuable.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

> *"The way to attack all these really gigantic ideas is to find the tiny thing that turns into the gigantic idea. Find the dinosaur egg... if you just made a search engine that the top 10,000 hackers used and nobody else, you'd have only 10,000 users — talk about poultry — and yet you would be in a very powerful position, just as Google was when they had those first 10,000 users."*

**Implication:** Founders tackling enormous problems should identify the smallest credible wedge into that market, particularly among highly influential early users, rather than trying to boil the ocean from day one.

**When building something for a specific technical audience, being self-indulgent and solving exactly the problem you yourself face is a strength, not a weakness. If you are the target user, your own preferences are the most reliable guide to what will work.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Founders who are deeply embedded in the problem space they're solving have an informational advantage and should trust their own taste as product signal.

**When powerful, busy people are in genuine pain from a problem, that pain represents a major business opportunity.** Such users don't care about interoperability or ecosystem lock-in — they'll adopt a solution that actually solves the problem, eliminating the chicken-and-egg problem that usually kills new communication platforms. ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Targeting high-status, high-pain early adopters in a new communication product eliminates the cold-start problem and creates social proof that drives broader adoption.

**Hollywood's big strategic mistake was being slow to embrace the internet as a delivery mechanism for entertainment.** The winner in entertainment delivery is now clearly the internet, not cable, and startups can attack this by figuring out how to deliver long-form drama via the internet at a scale larger than YouTube clips. ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** The entertainment disruption opportunity is not just distribution but the creation of new formats for long-form storytelling native to the internet, bypassing studio gatekeepers entirely.

**Moore's Law in its original useful form — where clock speeds doubled every 18 months, automatically making slow software faster over time — is gone. The new Moore's Law only delivers more parallel cores, meaning software must be explicitly parallelized to benefit, which is vastly harder. This creates a massive startup opportunity: giving developers back the abstraction of a single fast CPU.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Any startup that successfully abstracts parallelism away from developers — whether through smart compilers, better primitives, or human-assisted optimization — would capture enormous value across the entire software industry.

**A 'sufficiently smart compiler' that automatically parallelizes existing sequential code is conventionally considered impossible, but the expected value of attempting it may be very high precisely because of that reputation. If it's not actually impossible but merely very hard, the combination of high difficulty and high reward makes it an attractive target.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Founders should distinguish between 'conventionally considered impossible' and 'actually impossible' — the former category often contains the highest-value opportunities.

**One generative technique for finding startup ideas is to imagine how current practices will appear barbaric or primitive to people in the future. The gap between what we accept today and what future generations will find obviously inadequate reveals where real problems are hiding in plain sight.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Founders can systematically generate high-quality startup ideas by asking: what common practice today will people 50 years from now find shocking or absurd?

**The medical profession's resistance to proactive diagnosis stems from discomfort with 'incidental findings' — things discovered when searching for unknown problems. But this resistance is an artifact of current technical limitations, not a fundamental truth. As continuous scanning becomes more sophisticated, people will learn that most anomalies are benign, just as we've normalized knowing our weight.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

> *"A lot of doctors worry that if you start testing people all the time this is what you'll get — a lot of terrifying false alarms that make patients panic and require expensive and possibly dangerous tests to resolve. But I think that is just an artifact of current limitations. I think the way it's going to play out is if people are scanned all the time, they will know about these things early, and they will realize 'oh, that thing in my brain is just like a birthmark, it's always been there.'"*

**Implication:** Medical startups working on continuous monitoring should anticipate regulatory and professional resistance as a temporary obstacle rooted in current limitations, not a permanent feature of the landscape.

**Young founders with side projects are a strong signal because it ensures the idea emerged organically from lived experience rather than from a deliberate, inexperienced brainstorming session. Older founders can tackle domains requiring life experience, but younger founders need the side-project heuristic to avoid naïve ideas.** ([source](conversations_with_tyler:paul_graham))

> *"Part of the reason you want to find young founders who've done stuff from side projects is that it guarantees the idea is not bullshit... older founders can do things like start supersonic aircraft companies or build something for geriatric care and actually get it right. Younger founders are likely to get things wrong if they try and do stuff like that."*

**Implication:** The side-project heuristic is a filter suited to young founders specifically; it is a workaround for their lack of domain experience, not a universal law of startup ideation.

**The very best startup ideas almost have to start as side projects because they are such outliers that the conscious rational mind would reject them as business ideas. Yahoo, Google, Facebook, and Apple all started this way — none were intended to be companies at first.** ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"The very best ideas almost have to start as side projects because they're always such outliers that your conscious mind would reject them as ideas for companies. Yahoo, Google, Facebook, and Apple all got started this way. None of these companies were even supposed to be companies at first."*

**Implication:** The filtering mechanism of deliberate startup ideation systematically eliminates the best ideas; only ideas that bypass rational scrutiny and emerge from genuine personal interest are likely to be truly great.

**To develop a mind that generates good startup ideas unconsciously, you should.** learn a lot about things that matter, work on problems that genuinely interest you, and do so with people you like and respect. This process produces both good ideas and good cofounders simultaneously. ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"How do you turn your mind into the kind that has startup ideas unconsciously? One: learn a lot about things that matter. Two: work on problems that interest you. Three: with people you like and respect. That third part incidentally is how you get co-founders at the same time as the idea."*

**Implication:** The paths to great startup ideas and great cofounders converge: deep collaborative work on genuinely interesting problems is the unified strategy for both.

**The component of entrepreneurship that actually matters is domain expertise.** Larry Page became Larry Page because he was genuinely interested in search — the startup was the ulterior motive for the curiosity, not the other way around. At its best, starting a startup is merely an ulterior motive for curiosity. ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

**Implication:** Founders who start with curiosity and let the startup emerge from it will outperform founders who start with the desire to start a startup and then look for ideas to pursue.

**The worst way to come up with startup ideas is to consciously try to think of them.** This approach produces ideas that are bad but plausible-sounding, which means both the founder and others will be fooled into thinking they're valid, wasting significant time before discovering they don't work. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"If you make a conscious effort to try and think of startup ideas you will think of ideas that are not only bad but bad and plausible sounding meaning you and everybody else will be fooled by them and you'll waste a lot of time before realizing they're no good."*

**Implication:** Deliberate idea brainstorming is actively counterproductive for startups — the process itself biases you toward mediocre ideas that merely sound credible rather than ideas that are genuinely valuable.

**The greatest startup companies were never initially conceived as companies at all — they began as side projects.** Yahoo, Google, Facebook, and Apple all started this way, suggesting that the best ideas emerge from genuine interest rather than entrepreneurial intent. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Yahoo Google Facebook and Apple all got started this way. None of these companies were even supposed to be companies at first. They were all just side projects."*

**Implication:** If the biggest companies in history started as side projects, founders should be suspicious of ideas that feel like 'startup ideas' from the beginning — the best opportunities are more likely to look like hobbies or experiments first.

**The very best startup ideas almost must begin as side projects precisely because they are such outliers — ideas so unconventional that a rational, conscious mind would reject them as viable businesses before they had a chance to develop.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The very best ideas almost have to start as side projects because they're always such outliers that your conscious mind would reject them as ideas for companies."*

**Implication:** The filtering mechanism of deliberate startup thinking screens out the most radical and therefore most valuable ideas. Only by bypassing that filter — through organic side-project development — do the best ideas survive long enough to prove themselves.

**The three-part formula for becoming someone who naturally generates good startup ideas is.** learn a lot about things that matter, work on problems that genuinely interest you, and do so with people you like and respect. These conditions also happen to be how you find cofounders. ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** The conditions that produce good startup ideas and the conditions that produce good cofounding relationships are identical — by optimizing for intellectual engagement and quality collaboration, you solve multiple founding problems simultaneously.

**Technology can be thought of as a spreading fractal stain, where every point on the moving edge represents an interesting and potentially valuable problem. Being at that leading edge means being surrounded by genuine opportunities.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"If you think of technology as something that's spreading like a sort of fractal stain every point on the edge that's moving represents an interesting problem."*

**Implication:** The most reliable way to find important startup opportunities is to position yourself at the frontier of a technology — the problems you encounter there are inherently significant because they represent gaps between what currently exists and what the future requires.

**When you live at the frontier of technology, you sense that certain things ought to exist before you consciously identify them as startup ideas. The signal is a feeling of obvious necessity, not a deliberate entrepreneurial calculation.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Founders at the frontier should pay close attention to their intuitions about what's missing or broken — these pre-conscious observations are more reliable signals of opportunity than any deliberate ideation process.

**The best practical advice for young aspiring startup founders, distilled to its essence, is simply.** just learn. Everything else — the ideas, the company, the success — flows from having learned powerful things deeply and authentically. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Here is the ultimate advice for young would-be startup founders reduced to two words: just learn."*

**Implication:** The entire elaborate apparatus of startup advice, accelerators, and entrepreneurship education is secondary to the single discipline of becoming a genuine expert in things that matter — learning is the one input that reliably produces the outputs founders want.

**Wearables represent a more intimate and personal relationship with the user than the smartphone, because something worn is in constant physical contact with the body and becomes part of identity expression. This intimacy, if designed for correctly, could enable a deeper and more continuous data relationship than any handheld device can achieve.** ([source](foundation:27_hosain_rahman))

> *"When you're wearing something it's so personal and so intimate with you that it's a better and deeper relationship than you have with your phone."*

**Implication:** The wearables category has a structurally higher ceiling than mobile in terms of data intimacy and identity integration — founders who design for that intimacy rather than against it unlock entirely different product possibilities.

**The right question to ask about an early-stage startup is not whether it's taking over the world now, but how big it could get if founders did the right things. The optimal path to dominance often looks laborious and inconsequential at the time.** ([source](paulgraham.com/ds.html))

> *"The question to ask about an early-stage startup is not 'is this company taking over the world' but 'how big could this company get if the founders did the right things.' And the right things often seem both laborious and inconsequential at the time."*

**Implication:** Evaluating startups requires imagining the trajectory, not the current state — the activities that lead to dominance often look like nothing special from the outside.

**Startup ideas should be thought of as vectors, not scalars — pairs of 'what you're going to build' and 'the unscalable things you're going to do initially to get the company going.' This framing forces founders to think concretely about both dimensions from the start.** ([source](paulgraham.com/ds.html))

**Implication:** A startup idea without a concrete plan for unscalable early hustle is incomplete — evaluating whether you can manually recruit users is actually a useful filter for whether an idea is worth pursuing.

**The most scalable startup ideas don't begin with business plans — they begin with observations about human nature.** A nagging personal insight about how people should live differently is often the seed of a genuinely scalable company. ([source](Masters of Scale: How to price your product to scale (with ClassPass' Payal Kadakia)))

> *"The most scalable ideas require a leap into the unknown. They don't begin with a business plan. They begin with an observation of human nature driving a crazy hypothesis."*

**Implication:** Founders should treat their personal lived experience and emotional convictions as legitimate starting points for company formation, not just market analysis.

**Every consumer internet entrepreneur needs a theory of human nature.** That theory should underlie strategy, product design, incentive programs, and every major decision — the product is ultimately a bet on how humans behave individually and collectively. ([source](Masters of Scale: How to price your product to scale (with ClassPass' Payal Kadakia)))

> *"Every consumer internet entrepreneur, and arguably every entrepreneur period, needs to have a theory of human nature. Your product should be based on your theory about how humans behave both as individuals and as groups. That theory should underly your strategy, your product design, your incentive program, every decision you make."*

**Implication:** Founders who can articulate and test a clear theory of human behavior will make more coherent product and strategy decisions than those who rely purely on data or convention.

**It is much easier to build a product that plays to human vices than one that promotes virtuous behavior.** Vices — vanity, greed, lust — have hair-trigger responses; virtues like exercise require much more effort to activate. ([source](Masters of Scale: How to price your product to scale (with ClassPass' Payal Kadakia)))

> *"Most entrepreneurs take the easier path to scale. They play to our vices because vices are actually much stronger than the virtues. So, for example, convincing someone to floss or convincing someone to go exercise is actually much, much harder than to convince them to watch something scandalous."*

**Implication:** Founders building virtue-promoting products must work harder on habit formation and behavior design, but the difficulty also creates a more defensible moat if they succeed.

**When users try to game or hack your product in ways that indicate strong desire for it — like creating fake accounts to extend a free trial — that is a powerful signal that you have product-market fit and should consider building that behavior into the core product.** ([source](Masters of Scale: How to price your product to scale (with ClassPass' Payal Kadakia)))

> *"We started thinking about this, and I was like, 'Okay, everything is really actually pointing towards a subscription because people were signing up with multiple email addresses.'"*

**Implication:** Founders should pay attention to workarounds and hacks users employ — these reveal unmet desires that the product should formalize rather than block.

**Human-in-the-loop AI product design is the key to getting generative AI products to sufficient accuracy for real-world deployment. When the AI handles 90% of the work and a human refines the last 10%, you can reach the effectiveness threshold that enterprise users require without needing perfect AI.** ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"A lot of these generative products they can build in ways where a human's in the loop right so I can say you know I'm trying to build a job description for software engineer and the model can take me 90 of the way there and then I can come in and do that last 10 of tweaking."*

**Implication:** Founders building AI products should design for human-in-the-loop workflows as a feature, not a limitation — it is the fastest path to enterprise adoption and the most defensible product architecture in the near term.

**Explainability is the key unsolved problem blocking broad enterprise deployment of AI in high-stakes decisions.** Without the ability to audit why a model made a specific prediction, organizations cannot responsibly deploy AI in regulated domains like lending, healthcare, or employment. ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"The key thing is actually being able to explain why a model is making a particular prediction and then once you have that fundamental explanation you want to apply different lenses to it... you have to actually be able to explain why did the model deny what were the different variables and parameters around the data Vector if you will the applicant that led to that decision and is that fair."*

**Implication:** Explainability tooling is both a genuine technical frontier and a massive commercial opportunity — any AI application targeting regulated industries must solve this problem before scale deployment is viable, creating a wedge for specialized infrastructure companies.

**Data poisoning and model extraction are two of the most important security attack surfaces for AI systems.** As AI becomes more ubiquitous and high-stakes, adversarial attacks on training data and inference endpoints will become a major threat category requiring dedicated security infrastructure. ([source](greylock:intelligent-future))

> *"If an attacker comes in and actually you know poisons that data set you know you can create a model that's now making predictions that are anchored in a really adverse way... on the what's called Model extraction side which is you have a black box machine Learning System... and an attacker comes in and keeps hitting the model for different inferences with the goal of back taking out some of the underlying semantics of the data."*

**Implication:** AI security is an underinvested category relative to the attack surface it will present — startups focused specifically on ML security primitives (data integrity, model protection, inference monitoring) are addressing a problem that will scale with AI adoption.

**A certain kind of productive stubbornness — what PG loosely calls laziness — drives elegant solutions.** The refusal to do something the hard, conventional way forces you to find a better path. This is not laziness in the ordinary sense but a principled aversion to unnecessary effort. ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

**Implication:** Constraints imposed by unwillingness to follow conventional paths can be generative — the desire to avoid the standard approach forces creative problem-solving that produces genuinely better solutions.

**The 'mark as unread' email behavior is a signal of a deeper misuse of the inbox — people are using email as a to-do list. The surface feature request reveals the underlying structural problem that the product should actually solve.** ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

> *"If you were asking someone about email they'd say I like you know I it's really important to me to be able to mark emails as unread... that whole unread thing is a sign that the whole inbox is being misused. What's really going on is it's a to-do list."*

**Implication:** User feature requests are often diagnostic signals pointing to deeper behavioral needs — the right response is not always to build the requested feature but to understand and address the underlying workflow failure.

**Startup ideas are not light-bulb moments.** They begin as vague inklings — a sense that something might be possible that hasn't been done — and the outcome is uncertain and often implausible at the start. Representing ideas as sudden revelations distorts how innovation actually works. ([source](conversation:paul-graham-geoff-ralston-startup-school))

> *"I always hate it when people represent startup ideas as light bulbs, because as well as being the most clichéd metaphor imaginable it's also false. You don't just like have this idea and then realize it. It's more like you have this sort of inkling that something you could do something that somebody else hasn't done before and it's probably a bad idea."*

**Implication:** Founders should not wait for certainty or a crystallized idea — acting on a vague, even implausible inkling is often the right move, because clarity comes through doing, not thinking.

**The best startup ideas grow authentically out of the founder's own life and genuine interests, rather than being brainstormed or market-researched. When a founder follows a thread of personal curiosity long enough, it eventually collides with entrepreneurial opportunity.** ([source](disaster_proof_podcast_ep1))

> *"This is where the best startup ideas come from. They grow authentically out of the founders' lives. And more generally, it's not just startups. It's like projects in life. Like ideally, your work grows out of your interests in your life."*

**Implication:** Encouraging people — especially children — to pursue self-directed projects and follow genuine curiosity is the most reliable way to produce the kind of founders who identify real opportunities.

**The best startup ideas come from founders who 'live in the future' — people at the leading edge of a rapidly changing field who experience gaps before others even perceive them. Paul Buchheit's formulation, combined with Pirsig's advice, yields: 'Live in the future, then build what's missing.'** ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"Live in the future, then build what's missing."*

**Implication:** The most reliable path to great startup ideas is to become deeply embedded in a fast-moving domain. The ideas then surface naturally rather than being manufactured. Positioning yourself at the frontier is the preparation that makes the ideas possible.

**Very big startup ideas are psychologically terrifying, and this terror causes founders to avoid them.** The biggest startup ideas don't just seem like a lot of work — they threaten your identity. This is a crucial phenomenon to understand because the ideas that could make you a billionaire are precisely the ones that cause you to retreat toward safer, smaller projects. ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** Founders should learn to recognize when their resistance to an idea is fear rather than reason, and deliberately lean into that discomfort as a signal of potential magnitude.

**A creative middle path between full automation and manual effort is a 'semi-automatic weapon' — a human-in-the-loop system that looks like an automated product to users but actually has humans doing the hard optimization work internally. If that marketplace for optimization eventually attracts bots that fully automate it, you may end up having built the impossible thing without anyone realizing it.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

> *"There's an intriguing middle ground where you build a semi-automatic weapon — where there's a human in the loop. You build something that looks to the user like the sufficiently smart compiler, but actually there's humans in there optimizing stuff... if you ever got to the point where it could all be done by bots, you would be in the very curious situation of actually having created the sufficiently smart compiler, but no one would have a copy of it."*

**Implication:** Human-in-the-loop systems are a powerful startup strategy for problems at the frontier of automation — they deliver value immediately while the underlying automation catches up.

**Email was designed as a messaging protocol but has become a to-do list protocol — and it's a terrible one because anyone in the world can add items to your list without your consent. The right fix isn't tweaking the inbox; it requires a new protocol that gives more power to the recipient.** ([source](keynote:pycon_paul_graham))

**Implication:** The email opportunity is not incremental improvement but a fundamental rethinking of the protocol — building something that treats communication as task management with recipient-controlled permissions.

**Early product ideas often emerge from noticing a specific, recurring limitation in tools you use daily — not from abstract brainstorming. The insight for a startup can come from a frustration experienced personally as a user.** ([source](youtube:Nozomio_Arlan))

> *"What really every single coding agent lacks and just like agent in general is having up-to-date knowledge base what is happening around them. So when you use cursor for example... cursor will build that for you and it will access the web but it will still hallucinate because it doesn't have like this up-to-date training data."*

**Implication:** The most tractable startup ideas come from problems you personally encounter repeatedly — the specificity of lived frustration is more valuable than market research.

**Technology can be thought of as a fractal stain spreading across the world, where every point on the advancing edge represents an interesting problem. Getting yourself to the leading edge of some technology — living in the future — is a reliable way to encounter ideas that seem obvious to you but prescient to everyone else.** ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"If you think of technology as something that's spreading like a sort of fractal stain, every point on the edge that's moving represents an interesting problem... one guaranteed way to turn your mind into the type that startup ideas form in unconsciously is to get yourself to the leading edge of some technology — to, as Paul Buchheit put it, live in the future."*

**Implication:** The most reliable startup idea generation strategy is to become genuinely expert at something on the frontier of what's technically possible, where you'll naturally encounter problems that others can't yet see.

**What you need to succeed in a startup is not expertise in startups — it is expertise in your own users.** Mark Zuckerberg succeeded not because he understood startups but because he understood his users deeply, despite being a complete novice at startup mechanics. ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"What you need to succeed in a startup is not expertise in startups... what you need is expertise in your own users. Mark Zuckerberg did not succeed in Facebook because he was an expert in startups. He succeeded despite being a complete Noob at startups because he understood his users very well."*

**Implication:** Founders should invest their learning time in deeply understanding their users and domain rather than mastering fundraising mechanics, legal structures, or startup process.

**Domain expertise doesn't have to be technical — it can be design, organizational skill, or deep industry knowledge.** What matters is that you are genuinely stretching yourself in work that builds real capability, whatever the domain. ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"What was special about Brian Chesky and Joe Gebbia from Airbnb was not that they were experts in technology. They went to art school. They were experts in design and perhaps even more importantly they were really good at organizing people and getting projects done. So you don't have to work on technology per se so long as you work on things that stretch you."*

**Implication:** The 'learn to code' advice for aspiring founders is too narrow; deep expertise in any domain that produces genuine insight into user needs can be the foundation for a great startup.

**The right approach to generating startup ideas is to train your mind to notice them unconsciously rather than hunting for them deliberately. You want to become the kind of person whose background mental processes surface startup ideas naturally, often without initially recognizing them as such.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"The way to come up with good startup ideas is to take a step back instead of trying to make a conscious effort to think of startup ideas turn your brain into the type that has startup ideas unconsciously in fact so unconsciously that you don't even realize at first that there's startup ideas."*

**Implication:** Startup idea generation is a byproduct of becoming a certain kind of person with certain habits of mind, not a deliberate creative exercise — the process is identity-level, not task-level.

**The way to get good startup ideas is not to try to think of startup ideas.** Deliberately brainstorming ideas produces ideas that are bad but plausible-sounding — convincing enough to waste significant time on. The best startup ideas arise unconsciously, as side projects, from people deeply immersed in a domain. ([source](youtube:f4_14pZlJBs))

> *"The way to get startup ideas is not to try to think of startup ideas... if you make a conscious effort to try and think of startup ideas you will think of ideas that are not only bad but bad and plausible sounding... the way to come up with good startup ideas is to take a step back and turn your brain into the type that has startup ideas unconsciously."*

**Implication:** Founders should not sit in rooms trying to generate startup ideas; they should become the kind of people from whom good ideas naturally emerge by immersing themselves deeply in interesting problems.

**One reliable method for developing the startup-idea-generating mind is to reach the leading edge of some technology — to 'live in the future,' as Paul Buchheit put it. From that vantage point, ideas that seem prescient to others will seem obvious to you.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"One guaranteed way to turn your mind into the type that startup ideas form in unconsciously is to get yourself to the leading edge of some technology — to, as Paul Buchheit put it, live in the future and when you get there ideas that seem uncannily prescient to other people will seem obvious to you."*

**Implication:** The perception gap between someone living at the technological frontier and someone in the mainstream is the space where startup ideas live — founders who live in the future see needs before others recognize they exist.

**The best startup ideas look like bad ideas to most people at the time.** Examples include writing software for a tiny hobbyist computer or letting strangers rent airbeds on their floors — both looked absurd, but the founders knew otherwise. This is a necessary feature, not a bug, because if an idea seemed obviously good, competitors would already be executing it. ([source](How to Think for Yourself))

**Implication:** When evaluating startup ideas, widespread skepticism from smart people can be a positive signal rather than a reason to abandon the idea.

**When building something for others, the most reliable method is to build what you yourself want.** Writing the story you want to read and building the tool you want to use both ensures genuine desire is driving quality and provides an initial audience of similarly-minded people. Trying to serve an imagined sophisticated audience instead leads you astray. ([source](How to Do Great Work))

> *"If you're making something for people, make sure it's something they actually want. The best way to do this is to make something you yourself want. Write the story you want to read; build the tool you want to use."*

**Implication:** Founders who build for a hypothetical 'sophisticated user' rather than themselves often ship hollow products. Founder-as-user is one of the strongest early product validation signals.

**People systematically choose problems that are too fashionable and overlook problems that are unfashionable or dismissed. Overlooked ideas often hide in plain sight — in what has been ignored, minimized, or prematurely closed. Contrarian problem selection is a structural advantage.** ([source](Essay Breakdown: How To Do Great Work))

> *"People tend to be overly conservative in choosing problems to solve — seek unfashionable and overlooked problems. Overlooked ideas often hide in plain sight. Explore what's been dismissed, looked over, and left unexplored."*

**Implication:** Founders should actively map the graveyard of dismissed ideas in their domain. Problems that smart people have given up on — not problems that no one has tried — are often where the highest-leverage opportunities sit.

**Good startup ideas serve a small number of people who want the product urgently, not a large number who want it mildly.** You can either build something many people want a little, or something few people want a lot — and you should choose the latter. Graham visualizes this as a 'well': narrow and deep rather than broad and shallow. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"You can either build something a large number of people want a small amount, or something a small number of people want a large amount. Choose the latter."*

**Implication:** Early-stage founders should resist the temptation to broaden their appeal. Serving a tiny group with intense need is the correct starting point — breadth comes later as a byproduct of depth.

**The key diagnostic question for any startup idea is.** who wants this right now, so urgently they'd use a crappy version one from a two-person startup they've never heard of? If you can't name that person, the idea is probably bad. Urgency of need, not size of market, is the first filter. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"When you have an idea for a startup, ask yourself: who wants this right now? Who wants this so much that they'll use it even when it's a crappy version one made by a two-person startup they've never heard of?"*

**Implication:** Before writing a line of code, founders should be able to describe a specific, real person who desperately needs their product today. Abstract market sizes are irrelevant at the idea stage.

**Successful founders don't 'think up' startup ideas — they 'notice' them.** The biggest startups grew out of things founders built because they saw a gap in the world, not because they sat down to brainstorm a company. Apple, Yahoo, Google, and Facebook were not even supposed to be companies at first. ([source](How to Get Startup Ideas))

> *"The verb you want to be using with respect to startup ideas is not 'think up' but 'notice.' At YC we call ideas that grow naturally out of the founders' own experiences 'organic' startup ideas. The most successful startups almost all begin this way."*

**Implication:** Founders should cultivate observation and curiosity in their domain rather than scheduled idea-generation exercises. The posture is receptive and attentive, not generative and forced.

**Startup ideas should be thought of as pairs.** what you're going to build, plus the unscalable thing you're going to do initially to get it going. Both dimensions require imagination and deliberate effort from founders. ([source](Founders - Paul Graham))

> *"The need to do something unscalably laborious to get started is so nearly universal that it might be a good idea to stop thinking of startup ideas as scalars. Instead we should try thinking of them as pairs of what you're going to build, plus the unscalable thing(s) you're going to do initially to get the company going."*

**Implication:** When developing a startup idea, founders should explicitly plan the unscalable launch strategy alongside the product vision. 'Do things that don't scale' isn't just a tactic — it's a required component of the idea itself.

**The early wearables category in the early 2010s resembled mobile phones in the early 1990s — clearly directionally important, interesting to enthusiasts, but not yet capable of delivering the killer application that would drive mass adoption. Recognizing where a category sits in this maturation arc is essential for timing entry and investment.** ([source](foundation:27_hosain_rahman))

**Implication:** Founders and investors in emerging hardware categories should study the mobile phone adoption curve carefully — the right positioning is often 'this is clearly the future, but the killer app hasn't arrived yet' rather than pretending it already has.

**Market size may be the biggest determinant of startup outcomes, but smart founders and large markets aren't independent variables. Paul suggests that talented founders are more likely to identify and pursue big markets in the first place.** ([source](The (Actual) FR Interview))

> *"I think Marc may be right that market is the biggest determinant in the outcome of successful startups. But that's not unrelated to the qualities of the founders. Smart people will find big markets."*

**Implication:** Don't frame 'market vs. team' as a binary. Founder quality is partly expressed through the ability to recognize large opportunities. Investing in or joining high-caliber teams increases the probability of landing in big markets.

---

## Growth & Scale

**Graham has argued that the most important thing a startup can do when growth slows is to talk to users — not to run more ads, hire more salespeople, or pivot the product. Slow growth is almost always a symptom of not understanding users well enough. Getting back in front of real users and understanding why they are not sharing the product or coming back is the fastest path to re-igniting growth.** ([source](Before the Startup with Paul Graham (How to Start a Startup 2014: Lecture 3)))

**Implication:** When growth stalls, resist the reflex to add complexity. Cancel the strategy retreat and go talk to ten users this week. The answer to why you are not growing is almost always sitting in your users' actual behavior and words — not in your analytics dashboard or a new go-to-market strategy.

**Graham has argued that the correct response to slow growth is almost never to add more product features.** More often, slow growth is a distribution or positioning problem — the product is not reaching the right people, or the people it reaches do not understand what it does. Adding features to a distribution problem makes the product harder to understand and the problem worse. ([source](Before the Startup with Paul Graham (How to Start a Startup 2014: Lecture 3)))

**Implication:** When growth slows, resist the temptation to build. Before adding anything to the product, exhaust all hypotheses about whether the right people are even finding you, and whether those who do understand what you offer. Feature additions are often a way of avoiding the harder conversation about whether you are talking to the right users in the right way.

**Graham uses Airbnb as the canonical example of doing things that don't scale.** the founders personally went door-to-door in New York to photograph listings and help hosts improve their pages. This kind of direct, personal intervention produced the high-quality supply that made the platform work. No automated system could have achieved the same result at that stage — only founders willing to do the schlep could have unlocked the growth. ([source](paulgraham.com/ds.html — 'Do Things That Don't Scale'))

**Implication:** The most important early growth lever is often the one that feels beneath you. Founders who are willing to show up in person, do the manual work, and treat early users as individuals rather than data points build something qualitatively different from those who try to scale before they have earned the right to.

**Graham has noted that the Stripe founders, as an early example lauded at YC, would personally walk potential users through the integration process — sometimes spending hours doing what amounted to customer success work that most founders consider beneath them. This level of personal commitment to early users is precisely what created the initial density of enthusiastic users needed to generate word-of-mouth growth.** ([source](paulgraham.com/ds.html — 'Do Things That Don't Scale'))

**Implication:** The highest-leverage activity for an early-stage founder is often the one that looks most like manual labor.
